<script>
	function goToTopic(topic) {
		var topicMap = applySpecialCharacterMapping(topic); 
		var url = "/xpl/topicbrowse.jsp?topic=" + escape(topicMap);
		window.top.location.href = url;
	}
</script>

			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract --><div ng-if="::vm.details.abstract" class="ng-scope">
				<strong>Abstract:</strong>
				<div class="abstract-text ng-binding" ng-bind-html="::vm.details.abstract">This paper proposes a novel cooperative composite energy harvesting system that consists in the association of a traditional passive UHF Radio Frequency Identification (RFID) chip with an Electromagnetic Energy Harvesting Circuit (EEH-C). The objective is to exploit the i-v nonlinearity of the rectifier by applying a signal with time-varying envelope in order to improve the RF-to-dc conversion efficiency. Thanks to a multisource configuration, i.e., an RFID reader at 0.868 GHz and an external source at 2.45 GHz, the EEH-C is able to rectify the 3<sup>rd</sup> harmonic product of the RFID chip, in addition to the 2.45 GHz signal, without compromising the RFID communication. Additionally, digitally modulated signals are used at 2.45 GHz to further enhance the harvesting efficiency of the EEH-C. From theory, simulations and measurement it is demonstrated that the exploitation of the three nonlinear effects of rectifying circuits, i.e., (i) impedance power dependency, (ii) harmonic signals production, and (iii) waveform dependency can greatly improve the conversion efficiency of the EEH-C.</div>
			</div><!-- end ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract -->

<div id="BodyWrapper" class="ArticlePage" xmlns:ieee="http://www.ieeexplore.ieee.org"><div id="article">
<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>Radio Frequency Identification (RFID) is a wireless data-collection technology very popular in different applications and services such as logistics, manufacturing, access control and security. Sixty years after the publication of its principle of operation <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>, the RFID technology continues being part of the «« top ten »» technologies worldwide. The scope of the RFID technology is nowadays not only limited to the identification and tracking of inventory, but it is capable to collect and compile massive amounts of detailed real-time data in different types of environments around us. Therefore the evolution of RFID opens the way for a plethora of new applications in the area of Internet of Things (with 50 billion connected objects expected for 2020), Smart Skins, Man-to-Machine and Cognitive Intelligence <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a>.</p><p>This growing interest is primarily related to the significant benefits of passive Ultra High Frequency (UHF) RFID, in particular, its passive and wireless features that provide decisive practical advantages. For passive UHF RFID tags the reader transfers energy wirelessly to the tag by sending Radio Frequency (RF) power (at 868 MHz in Europe and in 902-926 MHz band in US) that the tag must collect and transform into dc power to operate and respond using the backscattering modulation technique <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a>. Consequently the passive RFID technology naturally embeds the principle of Wireless Power Transmission (WPT) using an intentional Electromagnetic (EM) source.</p><p>However the requirement of additional functions for smart tags has strongly raised the need of additional sources of energy <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a>. The idea of the tag-sensor approach is to associate new sensing capabilities to the tag while it is still enjoying the identification functionality and all this in a wireless environment. Many studies have demonstrated the possibilities of this concept in different contexts, e.g., for industrial or urban areas, agricultural zones, or Body Area Networks (BAN). A wide variety of sensor capabilities have been also shown: temperature, pressure, humidity, deformation, crack width, accelerometer, chemical sensors, etc. Two types of implementations are used where either the tag integrates the sensor or the sensor function is integrated in the tag <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a>. In the first case, the difficulties reside in the supply autonomously the sensor via the rectifier circuit of the tag itself (very constraint solution) or via another energy recovery circuit. Among the possible energy sources, the natural or ecologic sources such as solar, thermal, kinetic are of first interest. However and always with an ecofriendly regard, the presented work only focuses on the EM sources and proposes a new and original cooperative powering system that exploits wasted EM energies. The approach consists in the association of a traditional passive UHF RFID tag with an Electromagnetic Energy Harvesting Circuit (EEH-C) that efficiently performs RF-to-dc conversion from an RF source at 2.45 GHz combined with the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">3^{\rm rd}</span><script type="math/tex">3^{\rm rd}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> harmonic product generated by the chip (so-called <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">3f_{0chip}</span><script type="math/tex">3f_{0chip}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>). Contrary to some works in the literature that present a cooperative harvesting operation by adding the dc outputs of different harvesters, i.e., at dc level <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_1">[6]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_1">[7]</a>, It is worth noting that the cooperation proposed in this approach is done at RF level, i.e., it is based on the co-existence and mutual cooperation of two RF systems (EEH-C and RFID chip) without compromising the communication function.</p><p>The proposed study covers all the aspects from theory, simulation and experimental results in order to highlight the three nonlinear effects of rectifying circuits exploited with mutual benefits: (i) impedance power dependency <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_1">[8]</a>, (ii) harmonic signals production <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_1">[9]</a>, and (iii) waveform dependency <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_1">[10]</a>.</p><p>The paper is organized as follows. In <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec2" class="fulltext-link">Section II</a>, the state-of-art and underpinning theory about the exploitation of the nonlinear i-v characteristic of rectifier circuits used in this composite system are exposed. <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec3" class="fulltext-link">Section III</a> introduces the original composite harvesting system proposed in this work and explains the design methodology of the system. <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec4" class="fulltext-link">Section IV</a> presents the results of an in-depth simulation study based on the multisource operation and the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">3f_{0chip}</span><script type="math/tex">3f_{0chip}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> rectification. In <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec5" class="fulltext-link">Section V</a>, the approach is validated by means of experimental tests using conducted measurements. Finally <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec6" class="fulltext-link">Section VI</a> draws the final conclusions and associated perspectives.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec2"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION II.</div><h2>Review and Theory</h2></div><p>The proposed work builds on the possibility of enhancing the RF-to-dc conversion efficiency of rectifier circuits by applying a multi-frequency excitation signal. The nonlinear i-v characteristic of rectifier circuits can potentially lead to an enhanced RF-to-dc conversion efficiency when they are excited simultaneously by more than one signal relative to a single Continuous Wave (CW) signal of the same average power, such as the signal provided by the standard RFID reader. This effect was verified experimentally in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref11" id="context_ref_11_2">[11]</a>, where the performance of a wideband rectifier was tested using pairs of CW tone signals with different frequencies and power levels. In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref12" id="context_ref_12_2">[12]</a>, an improvement in the read range of passive RFID tags of up to 24% was obtained by interrogating them using multi-tone signals with up to 8 subcarriers which were named power-optimized waveforms.</p><p>A composite signal of a number of tones, or alternatively a finite bandwidth signal with arbitrary modulation can be expressed mathematically in a general form as a (multi-harmonic) signal with a time-varying envelope. A time-varying envelope results in the presence of instantaneous power peaks in the signal and a certain Peak-to-Average-Power-Ratio (PAPR). An improvement in the rectifier RF-to-dc conversion efficiency using Quadrature Phase-Shift Keying (QPSK) modulated input signals was reported in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref13" id="context_ref_13_2">[13]</a>. Interest in the potential efficiency improvement by tailoring the transmitted signal envelope properties has spurred a number of subsequent publications studying the effect of efficiency and the PAPR of multi-sine signals <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_2">[14]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_2">[15]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref16" id="context_ref_16_2">[16]</a>, or modulated signals <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_2">[10]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref17" id="context_ref_17_2">[17]</a> and even white noise and chaotic waveforms <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_2">[10]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref18" id="context_ref_18_2">[18]</a>. In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref19" id="context_ref_19_2">[19]</a> an efficiency improvement compared to CW signal was demonstrated using a composite 2-tone signal which included two approximately harmonically related tones at 0.810 GHz and 1.580 GHz. In the work presented here, a composite energy harvesting system is considered which includes two rectifier circuits, one operating at the fundamental UHF RFID frequency of 0.868 GHz and a second one operating near its <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">3^{\rm rd}</span><script type="math/tex">3^{\rm rd}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> harmonic. The reason behind the choice of the second operating frequency band is two-fold, first in order to potentially recover energy from generated <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">3^{\rm rd}</span><script type="math/tex">3^{\rm rd}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> harmonic products from the first rectifier circuit, i.e., the RFID chip, and second to harvest energy from digitally modulated signals corresponding to existing ambient transmissions at the 2.4 GHz Industrial Scientific and Medical (ISM) band such as nearby Wi-Fi routers.</p><p>It should be emphasized that the efficiency improvement is dependent on the average power of the input signal <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_2">[10]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref11" id="context_ref_11_2">[11]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_2">[15]</a>, since the increased instantaneous peak power may drive the nonlinear rectifying element (e.g., Schottky diode) in its breakdown region which results in increased dissipation losses, or lead to impedance mismatch at the input of the rectifier which results in reflection losses and subsequently a reduction in the obtained efficiency. In summary, the actual improvement in efficiency is a function of the signal properties such as its average power and envelope characteristics like its PAPR, the nonlinear rectifying device i-v characteristic, as well as the input matching network of the rectifier and rectifier load.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>Composite Energy Harvesting System</h2></div><p>With the intention to profit from the nonlinear i-v characteristic of rectifier circuits in presence of time-varying envelope signals, a composite signal generated by a composite energy harvesting system (multisource) and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">3f_{0chip}</span><script type="math/tex">3f_{0chip}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> will be build. This section describes the composite system. The approach of using a finite bandwidth signal with arbitrary modulation will be later exploited (see <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec5" class="fulltext-link">Section V</a>) using the same composite energy harvesting system.</p><div class="section_2" id="sec3a"><h3>A. Frequency Considerations and Power Management</h3><p>In this work, the RFID communication is set to operate with a fixed carrier at 0.868 GHz as per the regulation in Europe, in where the frequency hopping mode is not implemented <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref20" id="context_ref_20_3a">[20]</a>. A wider bandwidth centered at 2.45 GHz ISM band is also considered. Indeed frequencies from 802.11 b/g/n Wi-Fi standards with communications channels located between 2.44 GHz and 2.46 GHz are exploited <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref21" id="context_ref_21_3a">[21]</a>. It is worth noting that the scope of this work does not consider the harvesting power management but only proposes an innovative cooperative harvesting method. However, some complements on the power management trends in harvesting applications are discussed in the following paragraph.</p><p>One point to be considered is that most of the ambient RF power densities to be harvested are very low and the produced energy usually is not enough for most continuous electronic functions. However if this energy is efficiently stored over time, realistic functions can be performed in discrete time intervals. Some groups are currently dedicating efforts to study and develop efficient techniques of harvested power-management <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref22" id="context_ref_22_3a">[22]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref23" id="context_ref_23_3a">[23]</a>. The techniques are based in the co-design of harvester and power-management blocks in order to transfer the energy with minimal loss to the energy storage element and to monitor the energy storage and provide charge control and protection for the energy storage used. The deployment of autonomous nodes including operation functions (e.g., identification, moisture or temperature sensing, etc. <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref24" id="context_ref_24_3a">[24]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref25" id="context_ref_25_3a">[25]</a>) is possible thanks to the managing and monitoring of the harvested power. The harvesting approach proposed in this paper may be a candidate to integrate such a managing system.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3b"><h3>B. System Considerations</h3><p>The proposed system, so-called augmented tag integrates two sections: (i) the RFID section composed by a passive RFID chip and an RFID reader both operating at 0.868 GHz, and (ii), the Electromagnetic Energy Harvesting (EEH) section composed by an EEH-C and a Wireless Power Transmitter Source (WPT-S) both operating at 2.45 GHz. The schematic of the augmented tag is presented in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a> and explained below.
</p><ul style="list-style-type:disc"><li><p>The RFID chip is modeled by a 4 diode-based voltage doubler stages constituting a rectifier circuit with a configuration similar to one used in the Dickson charge pump <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref26" id="context_ref_26_3b">[26]</a>.</p></li><li><p>A dual-frequency Three-Port Matching Network (TPMN) is included to interconnect both the different sections in a common system. The TPMN matches the capacitive impedance of the chip in port 2 to the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">50~\Omega</span><script type="math/tex">50~\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> input corresponding to an antenna (at 868 MHz) and to the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">50~\Omega</span><script type="math/tex">50~\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> of the coupler that goes through the EEH-C (at 2.604 GHz). In the dual-frequency multiport operation context, a design trade-off is done in order to achieve the dual band operation of port 2.</p></li><li><p>A <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">50~\Omega</span><script type="math/tex">50~\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> directional coupler with 10 dB Coupling Factor (CF) is used to combine the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">3f_{0chip}</span><script type="math/tex">3f_{0chip}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> with the WPT-S signal at the input of the EEH-C. It is worth noting that hereinafter all WPT-S power configurations refer to the power injected by the source before the coupler.</p></li><li><p>The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">50~\Omega</span><script type="math/tex">50~\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> EEH-C is composed by a single stage of a voltage doubler and a matching network centered in 2.45 GHz. A first value of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm R}_{\rm L~EEH}</span><script type="math/tex">{\rm R}_{\rm L~EEH}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is as the load that allows achieving the higher RF-to-dc conversion. The value is driven by the load input value of the dc input of a RFID EM432 chip where the dc energy could be consumed in an application example <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref27" id="context_ref_27_3b">[27]</a>. The EEH-C design is similar to the one proposed in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref13" id="context_ref_13_3b">[13]</a>.</p></li><li><p>The RFID reader and the WPT-S are modeled by two <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">50~\Omega</span><script type="math/tex">50~\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> RF sources. Simulations consider for both, the RFID reader and the WPT-S, CW signals. Contrary the experimental part considers ASK RFID signals for the reader and different modulations for the WPT-S.</p></li><li><p>The propagation losses in a real scenario, in where transmitting and receiving antennas are used are considered by the parameter channel losses in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a>.</p></li></ul>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig1"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7343796/7327252/7327252-fig-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7343796/7327252/7327252-fig-1-small.gif" alt="Figure 1" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 1. </b><fig><p>Schematic of the model used to evaluate the benefits of the nonlinear operation of rectifier devices operating at different frequencies and cohabiting in a single system, i.e., the augmented tag. The RFID reader generates ASK signals and the WPT-s generates CW and signals with different modulations. In a real scenario, the channel losses represent the link budget of an RF communication considering antennas and propagation losses. The model used considers the following component values: RFID chip model <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C = 1.6~{\rm pF}</span><script type="math/tex">C = 1.6~{\rm pF}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">D_{1} -D_{8} ={\rm HSMS}285</span><script type="math/tex">D_{1} -D_{8} ={\rm HSMS}285</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{L chip} = 34~{\rm k}\Omega</span><script type="math/tex">R_{L chip} = 34~{\rm k}\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula>; TPMN <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">L_{1} = 33~{\rm nH}</span><script type="math/tex">L_{1} = 33~{\rm nH}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">L_{2} = 57.4~{\rm nH}</span><script type="math/tex">L_{2} = 57.4~{\rm nH}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">L_{3} = 115.7~{\rm nH}</span><script type="math/tex">L_{3} = 115.7~{\rm nH}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{1} = 0.1~{\rm pF}</span><script type="math/tex">C_{1} = 0.1~{\rm pF}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{2}= 0.1~{\rm fF}</span><script type="math/tex">C_{2}= 0.1~{\rm fF}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>; EEH section <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">L_{a} = 1~{\rm nH}</span><script type="math/tex">L_{a} = 1~{\rm nH}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">L_{b} = 3.9~{\rm nH}</span><script type="math/tex">L_{b} = 3.9~{\rm nH}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{3} = 1.2~{\rm pF}</span><script type="math/tex">C_{3} = 1.2~{\rm pF}</script></tex-math></inline-formula><inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{4} = 2.7~{\rm pF}</span><script type="math/tex">C_{4} = 2.7~{\rm pF}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">D_{a}-D_{b} ={\rm Skyworks}</span><script type="math/tex">D_{a}-D_{b} ={\rm Skyworks}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> SMS7630, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{L EEH}=5~{\rm k}\Omega</span><script type="math/tex">R_{L EEH}=5~{\rm k}\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7327252/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3c"><h3>C. System Design Methodology</h3><p>Advanced Design Software (ADS) simulation tools from Keysight are used for the design of each section and its integration to constitute a single system. Three main steps define the design methodology:
</p><ul style="list-style-type:disc"><li><p>The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">1^{\rm st}</span><script type="math/tex">1^{\rm st}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> step is the modeling of the RFID chip. Using the Large-Signal S-Parameters (LSSP) and Harmonic Balance (HB) tools, the RFID chip model is optimized at 0.868 GHz and for <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-9~{\rm dBm}</span><script type="math/tex">-9~{\rm dBm}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> input power, considered to be its activation threshold (EM 43235 RFID chip is used). Three main directives leaded the model design: (i) nonlinearity, i.e., harmonic generation and impedance-power dependency should be reproduced; (ii) the model should present a capacitive input impedance comparable to the ones reported in commercial RFID chips <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_3c">[9]</a>; (iii) under power regulations, the model should produce at least 1V of dc required to activate the logic part of the chip <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref24" id="context_ref_24_3c">[24]</a>. A first value for <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm R}_{\rm LChip}</span><script type="math/tex">{\rm R}_{\rm LChip}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> that allows complying with the directives is here selected. The dc management and voltage regulator for high dc levels are out of the scope of this work. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a> depicts the input impedance of the RFID chip model at its activation threshold; at 0.868 GHz the model presents <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">11-{\rm j}138~\Omega</span><script type="math/tex">11-{\rm j}138~\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> as impedance.</p></li><li><p>The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">2^{\rm nd}</span><script type="math/tex">2^{\rm nd}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> step is the TPMN design using the S-parameters tool and considering in first approach the other devices as linear in order to define its topology. The dual-frequency functionality of the TPMN with addition to three impedance matching functions represents the major difficulty for its design. Being the redirection of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">3f_{0chip}</span><script type="math/tex">3f_{0chip}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> towards the EEH-C the key of the proposed approach, the TPMN design is foremost driven by this priority.</p></li><li><p>The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">3^{\rm rd}</span><script type="math/tex">3^{\rm rd}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> step is the system integration performing the TPMN optimization. This step uses LSSP and HB tools and considers the nonlinearity of the RFID chip and EEH-C. The optimization goal is to satisfy the 1 V requirement at the RFID chip dc output and to maximize the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">3f_{0chip}</span><script type="math/tex">3f_{0chip}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> level at the EEH-C input. Two limit configurations of the power injected into node 1 of <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a> are defined: <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">Power_{1}</span><script type="math/tex">Power_{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> represents the power that allows activating the augmented tag, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">Power_{2}</span><script type="math/tex">Power_{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> represents the power when the reader transmits the maximum allowed by regulations <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref28" id="context_ref_28_3c">[28]</a>. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 3</a> shows the dc output of the RFID in function of the power delivered by the WPT-S and for the two reader power configurations injected into node 1. The 1 V dc voltage need is satisfied and its output does not depends on the WPT-S power, then validating the correct operation of the TPMN.</p></li></ul>From the presented methodology, the last optimization step leads to the optimal component values of the system detailed in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a>. <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a> presents the reflection coefficient and quality factor <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">Q</span><script type="math/tex">Q</script></tex-math></inline-formula> of the optimized TPMN. The dual band matching trade-off at port 2 presents a slightly diminished reflection coefficient at 868 MHz, i.e., <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-5.8~{\rm dB}</span><script type="math/tex">-5.8~{\rm dB}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, prioritizing the one at 2.604 GHz, i.e., <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-10.4~{\rm dB}</span><script type="math/tex">-10.4~{\rm dB}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The diminished matching at 0.868 GHz is enough to activate the chip satisfying the three modeling directives above exposed. From the presented model, the proposed full system in terms of the nonlinear exploitation is in-depth studied in the next section.
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig2"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7343796/7327252/7327252-fig-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7343796/7327252/7327252-fig-2-small.gif" alt="Figure 2" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 2. </b><fig><p>Input impedance of the RF model for a passive RFID chip. At 0.868 GHz the model has an impedance of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">11-{\rm j}138~\Omega</span><script type="math/tex">11-{\rm j}138~\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7327252/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig3"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7343796/7327252/7327252-fig-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7343796/7327252/7327252-fig-3-small.gif" alt="Figure 3" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 3. </b><fig><p>DC voltage produced by the rectifier part of the RFID chip versus WPT-s power.
